STATE RECORDS PRESENTS
While fully conceding the grave responsibil
ity assumed by a subordinate officer in will
fully disobeying an order of his commanding
officer, etlll .the' weighty reasons related, with
which Lieutenant Day was acquainted, so
tainted Major Waller's order that Lieutenant
I Day should have been prompted to positive dis
obedience. An officer must be conscientiously
regardful of the unquestioned legality of his
agency In taking the lives ofhia fellowmen.
Above . and beyond all personal consideration,
officers must guard the name and honor of the
country. Had Lieutenant Day been actuated
by such considerations he would probably have
prevented one of the most regrettable incidents
in the annals of the military service of the
United States. â¢ ' ;
The accused knew . that for three weeki
Waller had undergone a test of his mental and
physical endurance such as few men are called
upon to suffer. .
Giving heed ; to the mental attitude of Major
Waller, as much of the findings of the court
as are to the effect that Major Waller Is not
guilty of murder are approved. But the re
viewing- authority is . at a loss to understand
why the court did not find against Major
Waller In the minor offense. With the excep
tion noted, the acquittal, aa it appears in the
record of the proceedings, is disapproved.
Referring to the case ot Lieutenant Day,
the reviewing authority says:
The marines in Samar underwent (Treat suf
fering before their rescue and their officers on
their sick beds voiced the revengeful anger of
the men who telephoned to Major Waller, ad
vising that the stevedores be killed. Major
Waller received this message while he was
sick from a fever, suffering: acute pain of body
due to exposure and his exertions in behalf of
his men, and mental anguish concerning their
fate, which had long been in doubt. Major
Waller was at the time In teleshonic com
munication with General Smith, who com
manded the American forces in. Samar, but he
deliberately chose not to consult General Smith
regarding his contemplated action, and, rather
than forego the execution of his unrestrained
will, he assumed the jfower the . laws of war
and the' customs of the service confer upon
commanding officers only In time of war.
The sending of tlie natives In Question to
their deaths partdofe more ot unlawful retalia
tion than a "justifiable act of war.' In justica
to the American military service the findings
ot acquittal demand that they shall not meet
with unqualified approval.

Storm Isolates a District.
MACLEOD, Alberta, May 25.â The dis
astrous results of the five days' storm,
just over, have completely isolated this
district from the rest of the world. The
country has been flooded, rivers and
smaller streams are swollen to impassably
proportions and railroad and highway
bridges have been swept away. Traffic la
completely tied up on the Crows Nest
Pass Railroad to the Fernie mines. The
Dominion Government bridge across the
Old Man River and also the . railroad
bridge have been carried away. Other
bridges carried out are the traffic bridges
across the Belly and the Old Man rivers
at Willow Creek. The destruction to live
stock on the ranges is enormous.

Without detracting from the merits of his
brothers in arms, I can affirm that Rocham-'
beau was the flower of the' French allied army.
Mature in^'years,. experienced in military cam-,
palgns, a veteran of the Seven Years' .War.
calm, deliberate, self-poised, he was a. man '
according to Washington's own heart â the gen
eral on whose couns ->I and prudence he could
rely ' more than on that of any other com
mander. 1

members of the French mis

â¢ ' â¢ â¢ May the friendship Â¦ existing be
tween France and America â a friendship co
eval with our history as a nation, a friend
ship to which we owe our existence as an in
dependent sovereign power â may. this historic
friendship be perpetual. - May it be strength
ened and Â¦ consolidated by the iron bonds of
commerce and by the golden links of brotherly,
love. May the j two great republics of the
world go hand in hand in the march of lib
erty and nrosreca and civilization. And may
the monument erected to Rochambeau in the
capital of the nation ever remind 'the- American
who will contemplate It of the ever
laatinz debt of eratitude which the United
States owes to France. .,..-.*-
When the American troops under Washing-,
ton and the French forces under Roehambefeu
were on the march to Yorktown Washington
grallantly proposed -to give the post of dis
tinction to the French army. . Rochambeau
gracefully declined the honor, and In doing
so he followed the Impulses of his own heart
as well -as the general instructions- he re
ceived from his Government, that he should
always subordinate himself to the American
commander in chief. . ' . r
The army of Cornwallls was surrounded on
land by the French and American troops and
was prevented escaplner to sea by the fleet of
Admiral de Grasse; which guarded the Chesa
peake, and thus the defeat of the English
commander was inevitable, and he surrendered
his sword to Washington. The capitulation of
Cornwallls was the most decisive event of the
American revolution. ST

CHICAGO, May 25.â Daniel Hill, a
wealthy real estate owner and partner in
the firm of the George H. Philips Com
pany on the Chicago Board of Trade, was
chot and seriously wounded early to
day as he lay in bed at his residence.
Bix shots were fired, three taking effect,
one behind the left ear, one entering the
nose and the other shattering his right
arm. Considerable mystery surrounds
the shooting. According to Mrs. Hill she
and her husband were asleep when Hill
was awakened by the forcing of a lock
on the bedroom door. He reached for his
revolver, but before he could use It three
men rushed into the room and fired at
him The first shot awoke Mrs. Hill, and
she rushed to the window and shouted
for help, but the supposed burglars made
their escape.

PRETORIA, May 25.â The Boer deputa
tion Is etill here and the conferences at
Vereening continue. Nothing definite
as to the result is known, but it is
believed the negotiations are proceeding
favorably. At the. celebration of Victoria
day yesterday, a large crowd gathered
in the square here, expecting to hear a
proclamation of peace. There were no
disturbances.
A new feature of the South African sit
uation has been added by the establish
ing of separate republics remote from
Pretoria. Piet de Villiars has been elect
ed president of the republic, of Sange
berg. west of Vryburg. partly on Eng
lish and partly on German territory. All
of the usual officeholders have been ap
pointed.

Sj>ecial Dispatch to The Call.
Meetings of railroad men to discuss the
advisability of refusing to handle non
union and- bituminous coal I during the
progress of the miners' strike were held
in Wllkesbarre, Kingston and Ashley this
afternoon. .
The WilkeEbarre meeting: was more
largely attended than the one [ Friday
evening. It was stated that no action had
been taken. The meeting at Kingston
agreed to take the matter up next week.
The Ashley meeting indorsed the -miners'
strike and will render financial aid. The
railroad brotherhoods with headquarters
In this city, will alpo give financial as
sistance, but It is .said will not resort to
a sympathetic strike.
The strikers here are inclined to look
with favor on the action of the mine
workers' conference at Huntington "W
Va.. In ordering a strike of the miners
in Virginia and West Virginia. They
think. It will hasten the crisis in the an
thracite region. . .
Secretary Mullah of the Stationary Fire
men's Union reported to-day that the poll
oÂ£ the men employed at the various col
lieries has just been completed, and that
over 90 per cent of the men will quit work
on June 2 unless an eight-hour day is
granted them. Another officer of the
union, says the percentage of engineers
who will quit work will not be nearly as
large. The percentage of the pumpmen
who will come out is not known If the
local operators are to be believed it will
be small.

how many men they may rely upon to
work at the fires, engines and pumps after

_This was the quietest Sunday in the
AVyoming region since the strike began
The offices of the coal companies were
deserted, and at strike headquarters no
one was: on. duty. -.- _ _.
circular sent v out yesterday by
President Mitchell warning the Polish,
Slavish and Llthuian miners to remain
away from the bituminous region of Vir
ginia and West Virginia, where strikes
are now in progress, was read in many
of the churches. Some of the clergy
urged the strikers to be lawabiding and
be guided by their, leaders so long as
the leaders were -faithful to the trust
reposed in them.

NEW YORK. May' 25.â Anthracite " coal
may be mined! with bayonets. This will
surely follow. any, interference, with the
plans of , the operators who propose' to
conduct, their- affairs in their, own way.
At the' first indication' of serious violence.
In the ianthracite coal' fields, the o'perators
will demand the protection of the. militia
and reopen the mines. This announce
ment has" /UeeiT ifiaSe" By" a fiftaHCIar dOti
â¢cera_clpseiy affiliated with the operators
and fully conversant with the 'situation
from the operators', point of view. None
of the operators who could be reached to
day would admit that this plan had been
agreed upon, but all declare â¢ that the
strike would be of short duration.' They
couple this prediction with a reiteration
of the assertion that the operators w6uld
make no concessions. It has become
"known that tlie operators never believed
that there was to be a strike until after
it had been actually declared. Their con
fidence that trouble would be avoided was
based upon the belief that John Mitchell
would protect Iheir interests. There will
be no Violence at the mines until the order
calling out the engineers and pump men
who are now preventing the mines from
Being flooded goes into effect on June 2
It is anticipated that the strikers will
then do their utmost to prevent the places
of these men from being tilled with non
union men and that trouble will follow.
Presidents of the coal carrying railroads
are telling their friends that there is no
cause for alarm and that the strike was
inevitable since the settlement in 1900.
The operators feel that if they had not
been coerced by the exigencies of politics
they would have administered a defeat to
the strikers that would have settled the
difficulty at least for years -

CHICAGO, May 25.-Five hundred union
teamsters employed in the delivery of
meat by the big packers, went on strike at
midnight for ar substantial Increase in
wages and for shorter hours. As a result
of Lhe strike the men say no meat will
be delivered to retail dealers to-morrow
or at railroad stations for shipment out
of the city. The strikers claim that they
have received assurances of support from
the other labor organizations whose mem
bers are employed at the stockyards and
that any attempt by the packers to fill
their places with non-union teamsters
will result In a general tie-up of the pack
lug houses.
The strikers assert that committees
from them have been denied conferences
by the managers of the different packing
houses.

with vegetation at the top and with only
a cedar swamp . surrounding It. The

It to bo of volcanic origin.

The prisoner stated that he was called

ago and was on his way home, getting as
far as Chicago, when he was arrested,
pent to the detention hospital and after
ward to the State Asylum at Elgin. He
eays he remained there until last week,
when he escaped and came to this city.
He at once telegraphed to his home for
money to return, received it by wire an<l
was about to take the train for Hart
ford, he says, when he was again ar
rested and sent to the detention hospital.

SYNDICATE PURCHASES
KANSAS WHEAT ELEVATORS
New Plan Is Started In Hope of
Eliminating Middlemen and
Speculators.
KANSAS CITY, Mo.. May 25. â Walter
Vrooman of the "Western Co-operative
movement has closed contracts for the
purchase of six of the largest wheat ele
vators in the Kansas wheat belt and two
of the largest flouring mills. The prico
paid Is said to have been 1730,000. Vroo
man, who has left for New York to
complete the financial end of the plan,
say3 the present purchase Is but the be
ginning of a movement to center farmer3
of Kansas in a branch of the Vrooiaan
Co-operative Company. The farmers are
to be- taken, into the scheme upon the
payment of $100 each r for which they
are to receive the market value of their
wheat sold to v the company, and. In ad
dition, will receive one-half of the prof
it derived, the other half going to Co
operative stores, through which tha
wheat and flour will be handled. .
"The plan," said Vrooman. "Is to elim
inate wheat speculators and the middle
men. The farmers are in earnest sym
pathy with the movement. It Is the
only way to head off the talked-of flour
trust that is forming in New York."
It Js intended to ship to Great
Britain to be sold among- tie co-operative
members there, the surplus product be
ing disposed of In Kansas and Missouri.

Eighty persons who were arrested for
taking part In revolutionary disturbances
at Saratoff were confined In a private
building there. A number of rioters con
centrated in the vicinity of the building,
contemplating a release. The rioters were
charged several "times by the troops be
fore they dispersed and the liberation of
the prisoners was thus prevented.
Armed with daggers and sticks, the
townspeople, of Chirsel. in the Caucasius,
have liberated a score of prisoners who
asserted they were innocent.
Peasant political prisoners have been
whipped in Bielostook and other Polish
towns.

FLEE FROM FORT DE FRANCE.
Frightened Islanders Believe the
The officials in Fort de France have
asked permission of the French govern
ment to leave the city, but their request
has been refused. The troops on the isl
and are uneasy and frightened.
rne body of the American Consul at St.
Pierre, Thomas Premis, which was re
covered from the ruins, was carried to
* ort de France, where it was buried with
Imposing ceremonies.
Affairs in Port de France are reported
quieter, but the inhabitants continue to
llee from the- island on every available
vessel. Most of them go to neighboring
islands, but many of the citizens of Fort
de France have fought shelter in Trinity,
a town In the southern part of Martinique,
out of Mont Pelee's danger cone. This
town has become so. greatly overcrowded
that there is no shelter left and large
numbers of the refugees sleep out of
doors, on the sidewalks or under trees.
Provisions are being sent to Trinity from
Fort de France.
All the walls of buildings left standing
in St. Pierre after the first eruption have
been leveled by the succeeding outbursts
and now lie under vast sheets of lava. The
sea around the northern part of the isl
and is black with finely powdered pumice
stone and the trunks of burned trees
which have been carried out to sea from"
the mountain.
ports that Mont Pelee is still very
active. The whole top of the volcano ap
pears to have opened for a distance of
fully two miles, forming one vast crater,
from which ashes and lava are pouring
forth in enormous quantities. The lava
is running in great streams from the top
of the crater to the sea.

DISPATCHES from London report that an
unnamed member of the Ministry stated on
Saturday that the negotiations in South
Africa are practically settled and that the war is at an
end. It is explained that the delay ia closing the
negotiations is due to the difficulty of the Boer dele
gates in communicating with their followers, and the
desire of the British officials to give them ample time
to obtain^a ratification of their actions, so that when
the end comes there will be no reason for further dis
pute. It is therefore probable that some weeks may
elapse before the actual ratification of the peace
treaty, but none the- less the war is over arid King
Edward will have the satisfaction of knowing that his
empire is at peace on his coronation day.

PEACE IN SIGHT.
"Under the referendum clause in the
v charter the people can vote at the next
election on a proposition to make the li
cense tax any way from $200 to $600, and
I think the saloon men would be acting
in their own interests by agreeing to pay
$200 under an ordinance of the Board of
Supervisors."
"That proves," said the Chief yester
day, "that the $84 charged here is ab
surdly low, and no better argument could
be adduced for an Increase to $200, which
is less than in any other city In tha
State. I' can see no reason why the
Board of Supervisors should object to
pass an ordinance giving effect to my
suggestion, thereby enabling me to give
proper [ police protection to all parts j of
the city. It appears to me the only fea
sible way out of the difficulty.
In San Jose the annual fea i3 $320, pay
able in advance; in Sacramento It is $300
-or saloons closing at midnight and $340
for those keeping open all night; la
Stockton it is $200, but for saloons where
women are employed it is $600; in Los
Angeles it is $600, and In Oakland $400.
each saloon-keeper being required to give
a. bond of $1000 to conduct the business
In an orderly manner.
The suggestion made by Chief Wittmart
and backed up by tie Police Commission
ers that the liquor license tax bo in
creased from $84 to $200 per annum so
as to secure sufficient revenue to prop
erly equip the police force has caused
much discussion among saloon-keepers
and the tax-payers generally.
The Chief has been in communica
tion with the Chiefs In other cities
throughout the State regarding the li
cense fee paid by saloon-keepers, and
has received replies from San Jose. Sac
ramento, Stockton, Los Angeles and Oak
land, which show that the increase sug
gested Is very reasonable.

. CTJBAWT LEGISLATION.
The crops, upon which the business of San Fran
â¢ ; <co largely depend, continue to look well, and no
failures in any product of the soil are expected. Some
kinds of fruit will be shorter than anticipated, and
the same may be said of hay and grain; but taken as
a whole the harvest of the State will be large.
Conditions on this coast, subject as they are to
those in the Eastern and Western portions of the
country, show no particular changes. Like Wall
street, which has been tame enough of late, the San
Francisco market fo*- stocks, bonds and other se
curities has been inactive for several weeks, and, like
Chicago, this market for provisions and cereals is
without feature and quiet as a rule. Values for every
thing, however, comparatively high as they are, s<em
to be maintained without difficulty, and the domestic
and export trade of this port continues first rate.
There are no complaints from any quarter.though the
real estate men say that the demand for building sites
is not excited as for the past few months. Against
this report, however, the records show a number of
heavy transfers during the past few days, with the
usual announcements of large buildings going up all
over town. It must be remembered that real estate
Ii2s been abnormally active in this city for months,
*nd many handsome turns have been made, while
the tendency of values continues upward. As far as
that is concerned, whtn a town gets as large as San
Francisco it is a pretty safe proposition to buy real
"-tale almost anywhere ' within its limits, as most
-~>perty owners have learned to their profit and grat
'" -"lion during the past year.
Collections are reported good all over the country,
and the failures continue small and generally unim
portant, those last week numbering 192, against 180
for the corresponding week last year. The bank
clearings showed a loss front 1901 of 15.5 per cent,
the great cities of the country being about bal
anced in the matter of increases and decreases. For
the next two or three months, unless something un
toreseen happens, business will be more or less unin
teresting, as thjs summtr season is at hand, and there
never is much going on at that time of the year.
The other staples, however, are visibly quieter than
of late. The more favorable weather over the wheat
and corn belt and the promise of abundant crops of
both grains have taken the snap out of the markets,
and speculation in both has fallen to a low ebb. At
Chicago there is hardly anything doing, and while
the export movement from the United States is very
good, it is going along tamely and in a legitimate
way. Provisions, too, have subsided, and while
prices are abnormally high and the raw product in
more or less light supply, transactions at the princi
pal centers have materially diminished during the
past fortnight. Footwear is much quieter and sell
ers are making concessions here and there, in spite
of the fact that hides and leather have ruled firm,
with recent advances in the former. San Francisco
tanners, however, report -this market for leather
weak. The cotton and woolen trades are quiet and
featureless. .
LAST week was quiet in trade everywhere, and
there were few features worthy of especial
mention. One, however, deserves notice. This
is the strike of the coal miners, which a week ago
threatened to inflict serious injury upon business,
but which has happily been greatly modified by ad
justments here and there, so that at present it is not
such a serious menace and may turn out a trivial
matter after all. The railroads and iron trade, which
were especially threatened by the. strike, are doing
their usual business, and both interests report affairs
flourishing. The railroad earnings thus far in May
are ahead of those it the' corresponding time last
year, while the structural mills report liberal orders,
ivhich are running them to their full capacity, most
of them still being unable to promise prompt deliv
eries, and many being engaged for months ahead.
This protracted activity in iron and steel is remark
able. It has continued for several years and shows
no signs of slackening. As an indication of the con
dition of business throughout the country it is highly
gratifying. Â¦';â¢Â¦â¢
CONDITION OF. TRADE.
Three vacancies practically exist In the construction corps of
the United States navy. R. P. Hobson Is unfit for duty because
of impaired eyesight; J. T. McDonald is to be retired on ac
count of 111 health, and Lawrence Spear has resigned to enter
the employ of the Holland Submarine Boat Company. No
doubt the monotony of inspection duty at private" shipyards,
and routine duty at navy is not calculated to keep up the
interest of these comparatively young men; nor is the country
receiving an adequate return for the money expended upon the
education of these naval constructors. There is no naval power
having such an array of untried construction talent and there
is but slight prospect that any of our naval constructors will
have an opportunity to prove their proficiency. Under these
circumstances they can hardly be blamed for becoming tired
of a service that offers no encouragement to Individual efforts
and that they should retire or enter into the employ of private
yards.
Congressman E. G. Foss, chairman of the House Naval
Committee, made a curious statement the other day while
presenting the report on the naval appropriation. He alluded
to the fact that the Texas was designed by an Englishman
whom he met two years ago in Europe while visiting and
inspecting foreign shipyards. It Is true that the Texas plan.*
were designed _by an Englishman whose name was "William
John, but Mr. Foss failed to explain that the present chief
constructor, Francis Tiffany Bowles, then an assistant naval'
constructor, examined and endorsed the plans prior to their ac
ceptance by the United States Government, and that when hi9
attention was directed to certain defects in the design, which
subsequently proved to be serious, Mr. Bowles reiterated his
previous endorsement of the design and built a ship which
proved to be a failure. Mr. Foss' neglect to locate the re
sponsibility where it belonged was probably due to a reluctance
to expose the stubbornness and incompetency of the chief con
structor, but his alleged meeting with William John two year3
ago Is a highly Improbable event, for Mr. John died In Madrid,
December 19, 1890. *
That Germany is rapidly coming to the front as a naval
power is forcibly Indicated by the presence of a fleet in Irish
waters. The squadron, under the command of Prince Henry,
consists of eight battleships and three cruisers, and is larger
than the British Channel squadron or the French Northern,
equadron. There is a homogenity about the vessels com
prising this fleet which Is only equaled in the British navy.
The four battleships of first division are of the Kaiser
class, identical in all respects, size, maneuvring capacity, radius
of action, speed and gun power, and all of them are protected
by the same weight and distribution of armor. The fleet i3
practically new,- the oldest of the first division being only five
years old and the oldest of the others only ten years. In tha
second division there are some other slight differences, thÂ«
slowest ship being unable to make more than sixteen knots
against eighteen knots of the Kaiser. Taken as a whole the
German navy building is far more rational and systematic
than that of any other naval powfer, the squadrons of which
being as a rule composed of vessels with varying- speeds and
other qualities and therefore not as effective as a whole as
a squadron of ships identical in all particulars.
country than the British taxpayer Is likely to realize and
appreciate.

A first-class boy on the British training: ship Agincourt was
recently reported to the executive officer for having used ob
scene language and was ordered to receive twelve strokes with
the birch. Enraged at this the boy ran at the officer and
struck the latter a severe blow on the ear, after which a court
martial sentenced him to twenty-four strokes wijth the birch
and three months imprisonment. The corporal punishment
was carried out on the quarterdeck of Nelson's old flagship,
the Victory, where, according to old navy custom, the culprit
is stripped to the waist, lashed to a grating and receives his
castigation from a ship's corporal superintended by the mas
ter-at arms. The naval birch is similar to the ordinary besom
but is somewhat, smaller.. _ . _
'Five of Great Britain's armored ships have been transferred
from the effective service list to the D division, which includes
vessels fitted only for harbor service except in cases of great
emergency. The retired ships are the Glatton, built in 1S72; Iron
Duke, 1871; Neptune, 1878; Inflexible, 1881, and Swiftsure, 1872,
which latter is being converted Into a workshop. The total
first cost of these ships was $8,213,680 and $3,438,175 has been
expended upon them in repairs and . maintenance. * The Iron
Duke, which has been In active service on' foreign stations for
a longer period than any other armored shtp in the British
navy, was built at a cost of $1,043,815 and has consumed Jl 098 -
490 In repairs. The Inflexible, only twenty years old, cost $2*
659,230 and $689,060 in repairs, while the Neptune, with no ser
vice record whatever, cost $2,056,540 to build and $386105 In re
pairs. It is due to the persistent efforts of the British Navy
League to purge the navy list of obsolete vessels that the Ad
miralty has at last stricken ofE about a score. of ships from
'2ifi nrnhnhi * an l the savlnff m repairs on worthless craft
will probably cover the cost of a modern battleship every two
years. The Navy League has rendered a greater ser^icf to the
. The British battleship Russell has completed her series of
steam trials without a hitch and with highly satisfactory re
sults. The final test of eight hours under full power developed,
18,229 horsepower, 19.3 knots speed and a coal consumption of
2.09 pounds per unit of horsepower. The thirty hours' continu
ous run under four-fifths power, which is the severest test,
gave 13,695 horsepower, 17.95 knots speed and a coal consump
tion of 2.14 pounds. On April 18 a thirty hours' trial gave thÂ©
following results, with one-fifth power: 3768 horsepower, 12.1
knots speed, coal consumption 2.4 pounds. The ship was -2%
inches deeper in the water than her intended draught, dis
placing about 14,150 tons or an excess of 150 tons over her nor
mal displacement. The Russell is one of seven battleships iden
tical in all particulars and is 405 feet in length, 75 feet 6 inches
beam and carries a main battery of four 12-inch and twelve 6
inch guns. The hull and machinery were built under contract
by Palmer at Jarrow.
THE armored cruiser Francisco Ferruccio, recently
launched from the dockyard at Venice, is a sister ship
i to the Guiseppe Garibaldi built by Ansaldo at, Sentri Po
nente near Naples, and both are improvements on prior
types of which seven have been built during the past ten
years. The Francisco is of 7393 tons, 13,500 horsepower and
twenty knots speed and carries a battery of .one 10-inch, two
8-inch, fourteen 6-inch and eighteen smaller guns.
We' hope that Bishop Thoburn will so far reveal
his intimate knowledge of the supernatural purpose
as to kt us know how long we must keep it up, and
what it will cost and what the result will be. ;; ; -\
There is nothing irreverent in this spirit of in
quiry. Men want to know if -the. plan of creation is
so imperfect that all "this bloodshed and this killing
of the weak; by the strong are part of the divine
purpose to correct mistakes that omnipotence ought
not to make?
Bishop Thoburn avows his belief in the divine
fatherhood, and that is far beyond human fatherhood,
because it is omnipotent. By the unhappy accident
of prenatal influence, a human father may be
afflicted by a child that is vicious or idiotic. But
the races of men, according to theology, are created
and endowed with qualities, both moral and physical,
that were fitted to them before breath was put into
their ribs, or their ribs were made. A human being en
dowed with such power would choose to create man
in such form that it would be unnecessary to torture
and kill him to secure his belief in "any special form
of Teligion or his submission to any special kind of
civil government. That is what Bishop Thoburn
would do himself in the premises. Had he made
the tribes that dwell in the Philippines he would not
have so formed their minds and habits as to make it
necessary to create others to go there and kill them,
to burn their humble houses, lay waste their fields
and bring woe and havoc upon them. He is no
doubt too good a man to be responsible for such vast
and inconceivable human suffering. We do not be
lieve he would be that cruel to any of the lower
orders of life. Why then does he say that all these
things are being done by divine compulsion?
It is true that we read in Exodus that the heart of
Pharaoh was divinely hardened, but why may not
the hearts of men be softened in these higher and
better days? What great purpose is accomplished
by hardening the hearts of the Filipinos to their own
undoing? The Bishop admits that under the divine
compulsion we must go' on killing them, and Gen
eral MacArthur, a soldier, says that they are so in
capable of defense that it is like "killing children; and
he winced every time he had to do it. Complaint
goes up that the churches are quite empty on Sun-;
day. Is it because the view of the divine person and
purpose taken by Bishop Thoburn revolts human
ity and-the people refuse to believe in such a divin- ;
ity, and being told there is none other refuse to be
lieve in any? . V
If a human father find in his family one defective
child, who is mentally and morally weak, stubborn
and disobedient, he does not arm his perfect and
obedient children to smite that one .and wound and
torture and kill him, to force him to become as they
are. Such i thing woufd revolt the human ' heart.
The laws ma#e by man forbid.jt and their'swift'pun
ishment woufd follow such an exhibition of cruelty
and inhumanity. Now what is' the difference?
The views of the Bishop are very interesting, and
would be more so if any member of the committee
had examined him along the lines of his intimate
knowledge of the divine purpose. The unregenerate
mind is still found, unfortunately, and it will be
moved to inquire why an all-wise Creator brings into
existence whole races of people so imperfectly
equipped, morally , and mentally, that other and
stronger races have to be created to set upon them
with fire and sword to kill and wound and starve and
torture them into submission to the will of a Creator
who could have made them originally; so enlightened
that none of this cruel and inhuman punishment
would have been necessary.
He admitted that any Filipino who -favors Ameri
can rule is in danger of having his. throat cut^ which
reveals the fact that a majority of the people object
to our presence and hate our domination.
BISHOP THOBURN, whojias J>een on a mis
sionary station for many years in the Straits
Settlements, has been called -as. an expert wit
ness before the Philippine committee of the Senate'
His opinions seem to be derived entirely from a theo
logical view of our presence and responsibility in
those distant islands. He told the committee that we
are there be'eause of divine compulsion, and although
much bloodshed and .human suffering have been
caused by our going there, it is all a part of the
providential purpose.

According to Mr. Quincy and other promoters of
this public enterprise the conditions in Boston were
exceptionally favorable to its success. That it has
failed should induce great care in' entering upon like
ventures elsewhere.
The accountant's conclusion is: "Here is an.ex
periment in municipal ownership, a municipal in
dustry endowed with every advantage. Ample capi
tal, generous appropriations, plant in excellent physi
cal condition, regular work of the same general char
acter throughout the year, everything favorable for
a successful enterprise, which should do the city's
printing better than by contract, and which should
be able to pay as good or better wages, while giving
shorter hours to employes." The failure is declared
to be humiliating, and it is shown that the tax payers
will save money by abolishing the plant.
In addition to the waste, loss and high charges in
the printing department, the charge for ruling and
binding was found to be twenty per cent higher than
individuals pay, and there was extravagance in the
purchase of all stock used.
The expert printer concludes his report to the
Mayor by saying: . "The city is entitled to the same
returns from its expenditures that the individual ob
tains, arid this must be insisted on if the results shall
justify the expectations you expressed to me."
"The present hours of labor at the plant are eight
per day, with a Saturday half holiday, or forty-four
hours per week, against fifty-four hours per week by
the nine-hour day in outside printing establishments.
No one would object to this shortening of hours, on
the contrary all will applaud it, provided only that
the city's work be properly done in the forty-four
hours remaining. In fact, it is reasonable to expect
that more work should be done per hour per man
on a seven and one-third-hour day than on a nine
hour day, but this has not been the fact at the print
ing plant. The figures prove conclusively that, con
trary to reasonable expectation, less work per hour
per man has been accomplished at the printing plant.
These results have many causes, some of which are
designated in last year's report â but all of which
simmer down finally to two fundamental causes.
First, incompetent and sometimes useless employes,
who have been forced upon, or at least accepted by,
the superintendent; and secondly, that the necessary
vim and snap has not been imparted to the supervis
ion of the work. An unnecessary number of em
ployes has been carried in all divisions of the print
ing department. There isa lamentable lack of inter
est manifested in the work. In many cases the dis
position seemed to be to kill time, without reference
to the needs of the establishment. Hands were idle
for hours at a time.. This idleness is attributable to
the fact that the force is far in excess of the needs,
and were it a private corporation much of the help
would be temporarily laid off, or told to look else
where for employment."
The reason why this is a direct loss which increases
every year in spite of a higher charge, which would
itself be a satisfactory profit in a private establish
ment, is partly explained by the experts. They say:
The loss in five ' years' operation of the plant
amounts to $40,12883. This is the direct loss and
is increasing annually. For the first four years it was
$712944 per annum. The fifth year it was $061084,
This was the direct loss, the difference between outgo
and income. Added to this there is an indirect loss to
the taxpayers in the higher prices paid for the work
done. The experts found that on tabulation work
the price is twenty-five per cent and on plain work
ten per cent higher than in private establishments,
and in excess of the charge paid by the city when
its printing was done by contract. The amount of
this indirect loss is estimable only when the total
amount of city printing is known, and this informa
tion is not furnished by the report.
In discussing this subject we have heretofore re
ferred at length to the extensive socialistic experi
ments inaugurated in Boston by Mr. Josiah Quincy
during his service as Mayor* The chief of these was
a municipal printing office, in which he took great
interest and to which he pointed with pride. His
successor, Hon. Patrick Collins, has the same social
istic zeal, and with a view of promoting the policy
of public ownership ordered a comprehensive report
upon the operations of the printing office. The ma
terial for this report was gathered by two men, one
an expert printer and the other an accountant. Their
report is a sad disappointment to Mayor Collins and
the advocates of public ownership.
There are two schools amongst the advocates of
public ownership and operations of business enter
prises. One school 13 distinctly socialistic, the other
consults its prejudices rather than its philosophy. It
shares the feeling that is abroad against corporations,
and when shown that, as far as facts are revealed,
municipal waste is equal to or greater than corpor
ate profit, no impression is made because this school
of men seek revenge upon corporations and they are
willing to accomplish it though the method may im
pose upon the public greater burdens than those for
which corporations are responsible. The socialistic
school is calmly philosophical, and while it may be
as impervious to reason as the other because it re
fuses to consider both sides of the subject, its
optimism is more tolerable than the prejudice and
pessimism which are the motives of its associate cult.
THE revelations. of loss- and sometimes cor
ruption in the administration of light and water
supply by cities, under public ownership, have
i had a tendency to increase the demand for reform in
public accounting. Citizens who do not favor public
ownership and administration of public utilities are
found in the ranks of those who insist upon a better
system of public audit and accounts. As errors and
enormous -losses are discovered in the public opera
tion of light and water plants, the presumption is
not strained that equal losses occur in administering
ftie essential functions of government, such as fire,
police, school and street departments.
N EW FIGHTIN G CRAFT OF ITALY
IS AN IMPROVED ARMORED CRUISER
PUBLIC OWNERSHIP.
American interest in the situation is now centered
in the report that King Edward is willing to grant
liberal and generous terms to the conquered heroes.
It is said he has urged the Ministers to consent to
almost any- terms for the. sake of peace. It is to be
hoped these reports are true and that the new King
will show himself magnanimous as well as powerful.
Peace with magnanimity will adorn his coronation
better than all the splendor he can throw around it
though he ransack his empire for imperial adorn
ments. Even the coldest and least sentimental states^
man must see there can be no lasting peace in South
Africa unless it come with honor both to Briton arid
to Boer.
South Africa strongly intimated an assurance on the
part of 'the British Government that peace is at, hand.
A still more striking proof was; furnished in the re
port .of. the incidents connected with the recent
capture of 400 Boers.; It, appears that when attacked
the Boers niade no attempt to fight, but sought only
to escape. Thus there were no casualties on the side
of the British. That fact throws a strong light upon
the situation. It is evident the patriots of the Trans
vaal recognize that their cause is hopeless, and hav
ing made one of the most glorious struggles for
liberty in the history of the world they are now
facing defeat bravely and are averse to shed more,
blood merely for the sake of vengeance.

TACOMA. Wash., May 25.â To prevent
the Atlantic shipping trust from obtain
ing control of traffic interests on the
Asiatic coast eleven Japanese steamship
companies have formed a steamship com
bine with headquarters at Osaka. They
now have about seventy-five steamers in
operation, running from Japan to import
ant ports in Siberia, China and Formosa.
These steamers have a registered tonnage
of about 120,000. Several of the constituent
companies have new vessels building.
.Duplication of services at competitive
ports is to be stopped and all important
lines strengthened by adding additional
steamers. In this manner it Is expected
to get the Asiatic coasting trade thor
oughly under Japanese control. Both the
Japanese Government and the vessel
owners are anxious that this trade shall
be dominated by Japan. This does ! not
include either of the steamship companies
operating to San Francisco and Puget
Sound. Kach company joining has put
up a sum equal to one yen per registered
ton of its fleet toward consummating the
combine project. .
Will Oppose the Atlantic
Trust.
Eleven. Big Corporations in the Orient
JAPANESE COMPAITCES
COMBINE THEIR SHIPS
Before the chief reached camp Gordon
succeeded in escaping. He went to
Alaska, and recently returned here. Last
week he received a letter f r,om his In
dian sweetheart, dated Corpus Chrlstl,
Tex. She told him he had filled her life
with new ideals, and she could marry'
no one else. Resolving to become hi3
equal, she t learned English at a mission,
and - later * became a teacher in Indian
schools in Texas. She had waited in vain
for his return. Last summer she saw
his picture in a Denver paper. This gave
her a clew, and she wrote him in. Alaska,
finally reaching: him at Tacoma. Two
years ago her father died and she was
left a valuable estate, including a gold
mine. After receiving her letter Gor
don took the next train for Corpus
Christ!.
One day he met two Indian girls,* whom
he induced- to lead him from the wilder
ness. The prettiest of these became
enamored of Gordon, and to facilitate his
purpose of regaining civilization he made
love to her. This angered .the secend
Indian maiden, who hied herself off
and brought back a band " of Indians,
which seized Gordon and condemned him
to death at the stake on the arrival of
their chief. .
TACOMA, May 25.â Five years ago M.
P. Gordon, a miner well known on the
coast and in Colorado, became lost in
"Western Mexico. For eight months he
saw no/ human being, and lived on ber
ries and birds. When he reached civi*
lization he looked like a wild man, pos
sessing only the buckskin trousers, tat
tered coat and rifle of his former outfit.

a fractious horse. Lafranchi was return
ing with a sulky ,rake from the Charles
Avers ranch to his own place, and was
driving a young animal. With his limb
broken Lafranchi bounded from the sulky
and caught the horse by the bridle and
succeeded in quieting him. His cries for
assistance were answered by L. Bianchi,
who undertook to drive the horse. As
soon as Bianchi was mounted in the seat
of the sulky the horse moved forward
with such suddenness as to dismount his
driver and precipitate him under the
teeth of the rake. Bianchi was dragged
for quite a distance, and an intervening
ditch in the road is all that saved him
from horrible' mutilation.
Professor Miller took his master's de
gree at the University of Michigan in 18g4.
Late in 1884 and In 1885 he was a student
at the University of Leipslg, and the next
two years he spent in Athens. From 1887
until 18S9 he was a member of the faculty
of the University of Michigan. In 1S90
and 1891 he was a senior ' in the Roval
Archaeological Seminary at the Univers
ity of Leipslg. On returning to America
he accepted a position as associate pro
fessor of Greek in the University of Mis
souri. This he resigned in 1892 to accept
the chair of classical philology at Stan
ford University.
university. His reputation as a classical
scholar is well known and he is ranked
among the most- brilliant in this country.
He has spent a great amount of time in
study abroad and was at one time a cap
tain in the Greek army while studying at
the American school of classical studies
at Athens. He Is the author of several
text books, among: them "Prose Composi
tion, Based on LJvy,''' and also numer
ous classical works. Â¦ Â¦

At Fresno where a stop was made at
the request of the Chamber of Commerce,
the party was received by the following
committee: John A. Neu, president of the
Chamber of Commerce; George M. Boles
Henry Avila, Taylor Albin, C. H. Rlege,
C. T. Cearley. A drive through the raisin
belt of East Fresno was enjoyed and a
visit was paid to the St. George vineyard.
The excursionists will start for San
Francisco to-night by the Santa Fe,
reaching the city, to-morrow morning.
Luncheon was served at the hotel,
where a wealth of flowers had been used
in decoration. At the close of luncheon
E. Barris, president of the Board of
Trade, delivered a short address, and A.
G. Towne and A. H. Vail made short
responses."
In the afternoon the visitors were taken
in carriages again and driven eastward,
the high Sierras towering always in front
of them, through the groves and vine
yards between Dinuba and Sultana and
on to Orosi, a distance of six miles. All
of this country is Included In the 130,000
acres of the Alta Irrigation District, one
of the few Wright law district that have
plenty of water, no litigation and pros
perity. The diversity of resources and
beauty of the orchards and vineyards
greatly impressed the excursionists.
The hosts drove back to Sultana. reach-
Ing It at 4 o'clock. A special Santa Fe
train was waiting to carry the party to
Fresno. Everybody said /good-by, the
man from Boston broke in with "Let me
ask you one more question," adieus werÂ«*
waved and Tulare County's whole-souled
entertainment of the excursionists was
at an end.
The Dinuba people turned out en masse
to welcome the visitors, and the local
band played its. most enlivening strains.
Carriages were waiting and a drive to
the newer country of vineyards and al
falfa fields to the west was taken before
noon. This drive extended four miles
west, two south and thence back to the
town. The old '76 ditch, now owned by
the Alta Irrigation District, was inspect
ed. Among those who drove the visit
ors about were W. B. Nichols, E. Barris,
H. Nordheim, Emil Seligman, W. P.
Boone/E. C. Dickey, H. D. Hagen, Harry
Hurst, editor of the Alta Advocate: F.
H. Wilson. Edward Miller, W. P. Fulton
and G. W. Osterhout.
The excursion left Porterville at 9
o'clock. The special car had been taste
fully decorated by the women of Porter
ville and boxes of oranges and grape
fruit stowed in it for the refreshment of
the departing guests. At Lindsay and at
Exeter the hosts of the day previous
were in waiting with other boxes of their
choicest citrus fruits.
The run to Dinuba was made In an
hour with but one disturbing incident. An
excited section foreman held up the
train, mistaking the special car for tha
pay car.
W. B. Nichols of Dinuba, who had been
with the party from the time of its ar
rival at Visalla, and at all points most
attentive to the comfort of the excur
sionists, became host In chief as his own
town was approached. Charles J. Carle
of Lindsay boarded the train at that
town and remained with the party until
the departure from Sultana in the after
noon.
FRESNO, May 25.â The deciduous fruit
region around Dinuba occupied the major
portion of the attention of the San Fran
cisco business men's excursion to Tulare
County to-day. The visitors were royally
entertained by the Dinuba people and
were given a drive of twenty miles
through orchards, vineyards and alfalfa
field, the wonderful productiveness of
which was evident to the most skeptically
inclined.

It is understood that the vacant places
will be filled by promotion and by secur
ing new men. Probably Professor Fair
clough, who was prominent in the pro
duction of the Greek play, "Antigone,"
will be given the chair of Latin to succeed
Professor Pease. Professor Pease when
interviewed this afternoon declined to
make any further statement in regard to
the resignation than was published in The
Call yesterday.
CAUSE OF RESIGNATION.
â¢ This action caused bitter feeling be
tween Professor Miller, who greatly op
posed and resented the action, and some
of the others, including Professor Pease.
So great was the enmity engendered that
Professor Pease and Professor Miller have
not spoken to each other for some time.
This dissension in the department was un
doubtedly the cause for the voluntary
resignation of Professor Miller and
apparently for the enforced resigna
tion of Professor Pease. Professor
Pease held the chair of the Latin depart
ment and Professor Murray, that of Greek,
Professor Miller having the title of pro
fessor of classical philology but not being,
of late, the head of a department.
"This resignation," said Dr. Jordan,
"while doubtless the outgrowth of friction
within the department was wholly volun
tary and at ms own instance." '~;
The personal relations existing between
President Jordan and Professor Miller, it
is believed, are and have been very ami
cable. The main cause of the resignation
seems to have originated at a meeting of
the professors of the three classical de
partments, Greek. Latin and classical
philology, held two years ago, at which
a majority of the professors decided on
the abolition of the department of classi
cal philology, of whicn Prpfessor Miller
was the head. , .
Professor Miller is at- present in Europe,
where he has spent the college year just
closing. His resignation has been In Dr.
Jordan's hands for over a year, but had
not been accepted previously. President
Jordan when seen to-day said that Pro
fessor Miller had presented his resigna
tion some time ago as an act, in his judg
ment, "desirable in the interests of the
university." .
the university, and his resigna
tion has been accepted by President
Jordan.

FLYCASTEBS SUCCEED â¢
IN MAKING LONG CASTS
The clambake and sports of the San
Francisco Yacht Club at Paradise Cove
yesterday were eminently successful.
Several of the yachtsmen cruised up on
Saturday night from Sausalito and made
a big bonnre, around which they sat,
telling etories and singing songs until 3
o'clock yesterday morning. More beats
went up to the cove yesterday, and prep
arations were made for a big clambake.
After the alfresco luncheon there were
eports of various kinds, aquatic and terra
iinna. The swimming race was won by
W. H. Young, and the tub race by W.
W. Haley, ln addition to these events
there were boxing contests, partnership
races and tugs of war under the direc
tion of Professor George Miehling of
t he Olympic . Club. A blindfold boxing
bout created much amusement, as did a
fat men's race, which was won by A. G.
A. Mueller.
It had been intended to line up the
crews of the various yachts on the beach
and that at a | given signal they should
pull oft to their yachts, hoist sail, weigh
anchor and race down to their moorings
at Sausalito.
This part of the programme was not
carried out, as before the time for start
ing arrived, a gale had sprung up so
that some of the yachts dragged their
anchors, and the yachtsmen had . their
hands fuIL The yawl Tramontana, which
Â¦was anchored off chore, dragged for a
considerable distance. On' getting her
anchor up she sailed, away under bare
poles. Then a stay sail was set, and.
later, a jigger and jib. On the return
trip she ran aground in "a cove opposite
the quarantine station on Angel Island
on a falling tide, and remained there till
the flood tide floated her off. Nearly all
the yachts went out under double-reefed
mainsails.
Among the vessels at anchor. in Para
dise Cove yesterday were the flagship
Thetis, the sloops Surprise, Challenger,
Cygnus, Rover; Halcyon, Amigo, Sappho;
the yawls Tramontana and Phyllis; the
schooner Chispa; the sloops Anita, Violet.
"Witch, and the launches Edwina and
Rob Roy of the San . Francisco ; Tacht
Club: the flagship Edna, the sloops
Speedwell, Freda, Aeolus, Truant, Mig
non, Presto and the yawl Seven Bells of
the Corinthian Yacht Club, and the sloop
Occident of the California Yacht Club.
COLOBADO tiat.t, GAME
WTNDS TJP IH A BIOT
Negro Quarrels With. a White Man
and Narrowly Escapes Being
Lynched. ' -"4.
FLORENCE. Colo.. May 25.â A riot oc
curred after a baseball game here this
afternoon which nearly resulted in the
lynching of a negro named Jason "Wilson.
After the game between the Colorado
Kuel and Iron team and the Florence
Eagies. "Wilson, who was driving in a
buggy, tried to pass every one on the
crowded road. He ran into the buggy
occupied by Onnie Carroll, a local gam
bler, and the wheels of the two vehicles
became locked. Angry words followed,
and the two men got out of their buggies
and began to fight. The crowd closed
around them and began beating and kick
ing the negro. A half-dozen olBcers res
cued Wilson, but they had to draw their
revolvers to stand off the crowd.

The event of the day was the winning
of the Germania Club . bullseye shoot by
"William Garms. He is a veteran in the
use- of the rifle and the proud, owner of
many prize medals. The younger marks
men are fond of telling him he is a "has
been," but his 160-point bullseye yesterday
gave him the opportunity to say some
thing-, and * he 'did' not neglect' the chance.

were present yesterday at Shell Mound
rifle range. The /contests were all close
and honors were iiard to win. :

Flying Fox Beats Dewdrop
in Special Event at
Union Park.
After knocking at the door for a long
time Frank Jones put three winners over
the. plate yesterday at Union Coursing
Park.
Wedgewood and Lady Newark met in
the, deciding course, the former scoring
five points to the latter'a nothing. Tyrone
Prince was the third representative of tfcB
kennel to land inside the money, fiyi
bettors thought the Prince could outfoot
Lady Newark in the semi-final round and
laid 2 to 1 against him. The Lady won
out with a 4 to 3 score.
In the third round Lady Newark shut
out Musket, the 1 to 5 choice, pointless.
Mosq worked his way down to the semi
final round, defeating 1 among others Rural
Artist, a 1 to 2 favorite. -
In the special stake A. R. Curtis' Flylns
Fox^went through in excellent style. In*
the deciding course Dewdrop had a head
on collision with the Fox. The latter
was knocked down. There were several
collisions during the long course, which
the Curtis crack finally won.

tain Jones' valuables lost.; The remainder
of the Journey was a record of contrary
gales, rough seas and bad ; weather.

Few Face the Winds at Ocean Beach
and Drivers at Park Are
Scarce, f â¢'?$-.
Inclement weather kept the usual big
rrowd from the ocean- beach yesterday,
nor was Golden Gate Park much patron
ized by either pedestrians or drivers. The
ocean was calm notwithstanding the gale
blowing over It. and vessels came in and
went out gliding along as though there
>Â«s nothing more than a zephyr, so that
-wi*!: few who went to the cliff in the hope
\f seeing some fancy waves towerin?
mountain high were disappointed.
Sutro Baths were well patronized, the
people showing an appreciation of the fine
shelter of the great building. There were
peveral swimming events and an excellent
orchestra was in attendance.
There were no mishaps at the beach be
yond a frisky horse making an attempt
to bolt. Before It got many yards, how
ever, the driver had the animal under
control.

BY CALIFORNIA CLUB
Under the able management of Charles
Luttringer of 4530 Mission street the So
ciety of Old Friends met there yesterday
and enjoyed a bullshead breakfast. More
than ninety members of the organization
sat down to partake of the good things
that "mine host" had prepared for them
in a corner well sheltered from the wind,
after which speeches were made by J. E.
Slinkey, Harry Niemann, Paul Desmond,
H. Romef and others, and later on the
society was photographed. The officers of
the Society of Old Friends are: George
W. Kelley, president; J. E. Slinkey, sec
retary; Paul Desmond, chairman board of
directors. Harry Niemann was master of
ceremonies.

FRIENDS HONOR LITTLE
LUPIT A WITH BENEFIT
SAN JOSE, May 25.â The convention of
the representatives of boards of trades
and kindred organizations of the central
coast counties, which is to meet In San
Jose Thursday morning, will be an im
portant gathering and from which much
good is expected. In this district Is in
cluded Santa Clara, San Mateo, San
Benito, Santa Cruz, Monterey, San Luis
Obispo counties and part of Santa Bar
bara. At least seventy-five delegates will
be present, and these will represent every
element of business and professional life
in the section.
Business pertaining to the advancement
and advertising of the resources of the
half-dozen counties will be ! considered.
They will join hands and try to attract
investing capital and .settlers. All the
boards of trade, improvement clubs and
the like in the counties will be repre
sented,'and the interchange of Ideas will
benefit their, members. It will broaden
the possibilities of the work of inducing
immigration and attracting tourists to
the resorts along the coast llne.l
Delegates are expected to begin arriv
ing Wednesday evening, while those from
the South will arrive ' early Thursday
morning. The convention will be held at
Hale's Hall. So far as learned there will
be eleven delegates from San Luis Obispo
County, ten from Monterey County, San
Benito will send delegates from two or*
ganlzatlons, and the largest towns of
Canta Cruz County will be represented.
There will also be delegates from Red
wood City and San Mateo, while every
town in this county will be represented.
Thursday evening the delegates will be
the guests of the Santa Clara County Im
provement Club at a banquet at the Ven
dome. At least 250 will be present at the
feast, among them many San Franciscans.
in the Central Coast Section
Will Meet.

"Let us waste no time in needless intro
duction or preface. Within these sacred
walls Starr King spoke these words while
he served his God during four years of the
Civil war." In this manner the Hon. D.
K. McKinlay opened his address to the
congregation. "Let me do likewise," he
continued, "and get to the matter in
hand." He then reminded them of the
reason for these memorial services; he
spoke of the brave men who had fought
and died for a great principle and what
a priceless blessing the American republic
had received by these sacrifices. It was
good to think that the church set aside
one day in the year for memorial ser
vices, so that the people might pay their
devotions at the shrine of the heroes who
had died. He told them how the Puritans
themselves but lately free from the op
pressor had been the first to plant the
seÂ«Â»ds of slavery.

The money market shows a somewhat
firmer tendency, and the private rate of
discount reached 2V* per cent.
German state securities were weak and
required intervention buying to maintain
quotations.' The Saxon loan of 50,000,000
marks at 3 per cent will be subscribed

den. Industrials generally weakened. '
BERLIN, May 25.â The Boerse remained
stagnant during the past week and was
without new or striking feature.
Americans have certainly been stronger
during the past, week on the strength of
easier money In Wall street and the en
couraging crop' reports. The outlook for
Ncrthern Pacifies is much better than It
was, but the extension of the coal strike
in the United States is worrying a good
many operators. Canadian Pacifies were
strong last week and touched 139 on fur
ther New York purchases.
Buying, which lately has been restricted
to gilt-edge investment stocks, is now ex
tending to the more speculative Issues.
Home rails have made advances, and it
is known that many new issues of capital
are pending, and this knowledge renders
buyers chary. It is also known th?.t
about 2000 new loans and companies are
only awaiting a favorable moment to en
ter into competition with the savings of
the public.
LONDON, May 25.â After many weeks
of anxious waiting the peace announce
ment has finally crystallised into a strong
upward movement on the Stock Ex
change. In spite of the lack of definite
news, official or-otherwise, from Pretoria,
the market assumed strength on the gen
eral indications: that peace was assured.
The buoyant tone in nearly all the de
partments has been Increasing steadily
throughout the past week, so when the
official announcement of peace comes the
boom will probably already be in full
swing and every reasonable advance will
be fully discounted. As for weeks past,
the market is still Indebted to the banK
by over Â£2,000,000, while the slow-Govern
ment disbursement* jgndasithe repayment
oÂ£ this indebtedriess<l!nlcult. While the
announcement of peace is expected to
hasten the full resumption of work In the
Rand it is likely that the industrial re
quirements of the reconstruction period in
South Africa will absorb qufte as much
cash as the mines produce in gold for the
remainder of the present" year. There is
consequently â¢ little prospect for easy
rrioney unless gold comes from the Conti
nent. It is assumed that New York is
more likely to demand gold than to send
it here.

The amateur quarter-mile race proved
the most exciting event of the day. Fred
Berges won, Harry Irwin secured second
place and George Berges third. The prize
was a handsome silver cup given by Al
Pape. The success of the affair was due
mainly to the efforts of the following
committee of arrangements:
The company, In uniform and accompa
nied by numerous friends, assembled at
Turn Verein Hall and marched to the
ferry In time to catch the 10 o'clock boat.
The day's amusements consisted for the
most part in dancing, bowling and rac
ing. F. Lilkendey, the -new king, was
crowned late in the afternoon amid great
enthusiasm.
The San Francisco Schuetzen Verein
concluded its annual May festival yester
day with a family outing at Shell Mound
Park. The attendance was very large,
and those present voted - the occasion a
most enjoyable* one.

ARE BEATEN HANDILY
With the work of building the Pacific cable
under way with a -private company of un
doubted integrity and responsibility back of it,
it seems U> me that the proposition for the
Go\ernment to expend $10,000,000 or. $20,000,000
In this enterprise is unworthy of consideration.
I do not belie've the Government should em
bark in any enterprise which can legitimately
be carried on by private individuals. John w.
Mackay, the head of this company, is held in
the hizhest esteem in California, both as a.
business man and as a citizen. I have abso
lute confidence In his word and that he will
do whatever he agrees to do. -
Representative Woods of California
said:
Â¦ I have been for years anxious for a cable
acrofs the Pacific. ' I â¢ introduced the Corliss
bill, confident of its passage through both
bouses. I had an impression that the only
way to set a Pacific cable was to have the
Government build it. The fact has developed
that a perfectly capable private company has
decided to build, a cable without any Govern
ment aid and has begun the work. This com
pany, the Commercial Pacific Cable, has of
fered to build th^ cable upon its own respon
sibility with its own money, and at its own
risk, and as a fact is . now proceeding very
rapidly with the work. The Senate Naval Af
fairs Committee, to whom this bill was re
ferred, took uÂ» the question and found that
this was the situation. I then asked that my
bill be indefinitely laid aside and the committee
assented to this by a unanimous vote. If the
Government should either now or hereafter de
sire to own a cable across the Pacific the only
reasonable thine for the Government ' to do ia
to let this company build the cable and then
bay it. /
Representative Loud of California, an
speaking to-day on . the same subject,
Eaid: ' Â¦.. Â¦ ; \

Special Dispatch to The Call.
Faulkner was a fireman on the South
ern Pacific up to a couple of years ago,
when he lost his position through gam
bling and the consequent garnishment of
his pay check. Since that time there
had been an estrangement between
Faulkner and his wife. Recently she be
gan arrangements to sue for a divorce.
Faulkner heard of her intended action,
and made the remark that if his wife
did not come back to live with him sne
would never live with any other man,'
but-no attention was paid to the threat.
To-day as Mrs. Faulicner was crossing
the track to call on a neighbor, wheeling
their ten-month-old baby in a carriage,
che was met by Faulkner. They had a
short conversation, but of what import
is not known. The first intimation of
trouble was when Faulkner struck his
wife. She grabbed the baby out of the
carriage, and as she did so Faulkner
pulled a revolver and commenced shoot
ing. The first shot went wild, but the
second struck Mrs. Faulkner in the up
p^r part of her right arm and shattered
the bone. The third shot entered her
hip and ranged downward, while a fourth
bullet entered near the small of the beck,
and, ranging upward, passed through her.
heart. He then turned the revolver upon
himself and fired a bullet into his right
temple, dying almost instantly. The face
of the baby was powder burned, but oth
erwise the -child was not injured.
OGDEJf, Utah, May 23.â Ren Faulkner.
an ex-Southern Pacific Railroad man,
shot and killed his wife and then com
mitted suicide this afternoon on the
Rio Grande Railroad tracks in the north
end of the Ogden yards. Jealousy was
the cause of the double crime.

The annual anniversary meeting of the
University Christian Association was held
in the chapel to-night.' The meeting was
particularly well attended. The Rev. C.
R. Brown of Oakland spoke on "The
Message of Religion to the Men of To
day."
"Finally, let us dwell upon the structure
of that fourth pillar, which completes the
'star' of Emerson, in the essential ele
ment of Reverence. The age is not a rev
erent one; it cannot be until reverence Is
nursed In the conservatory of the home.
Parental restraint Is scoffed at by chil
dren barely in their' teens. Where the first
lessons of reverence are not home lessons
the spirit of reverence will likely pass
from home to school, to college and to
church."
"Taking the third pillar that supports
the temple of true Christian character, it
will be found in the doctrine of Annihila
tionâself-annihilation in service. The suc
cess which has attended you in your uni
versity career has been in. proportion as
j'ou have lost yourself in your subject.
Now as you go forth into the fulfillment
of your life purpose, just as you experi
erce this process of self-annihilation,
even so will your life work prosper.
FIRST LESSONS OF REVERENCE.
"Passing from the first letter of 'star'
ia the ideals of sanctification, we shall
consider the second pillar in the qualities
included in the spirit of Thoroughness.
Search where you will, there is not a
page in history that pictures such a thor
ough life as that which the gospels de
pict in the life of the son of God. No
where is the lesson of duty taught with
euch sublime force. This is an age of in
tenseness, -of unremitting struggle, in
which we almost idolize that quality
known as 'the strenuous life.' And who
knows It better than those who between
the ages of 20 and 30 are learning that the
only*genius Is the genius of hard work;
who toil on with ambitious flame to make
a mark for themselves in the world? I
ara confident that I stand this day before
hundreds of such lives who would hail
the encomiums of the professor, 'There
goes a thorough student,' with that just
pride that takes possession of the British
soldier's heart as he clasps the Victoria
cross. To you who go out from these
walls to make your record the burning
words of the great, strong thinker, Rob
ertson, when dying, 'It is finished,' must
come home with special force. To dig
deep down to the bedrock of your better
life, to build a strong foundation of a
Christlike faith, to erect a temple of life's
tasks in your profession as a citizen, as
a Christian gentleman and gentlewoman,
to do It well, to do it thoroughly.
LESSON OF DUTY.
"This method of viewing the foundation
of true character is a libel against Chris
tianity. A holy man is a wholesome man.
Sanctiflcation is good health. It enters
the very holy of holies of a man's being.
It involves a self-controlâ that royal
thing without which there can be no real
strength; the capacity to say 'I will' by
the grace of God when there is need of
a strong negation.
"When that great mind of New Eng
land, Emerson, urged the cultivation of
lofty aims his well-known counsel clinched
his conception of the true ideal in the
words, 'Hitch your wagon to a star.' I
take that word 'star,' and with the hope
of aiding the memory, what each letter
of the word stands forâ sanctiflcation.
There is not perhaps a word In the whole
range of theology more misunderstood
and misquoted than the word 'sanctify.'
It would appear that the responsibility
for its misuse rests quite as much upon
the Christian as upon the non-Christian
world. I will venture to say that if I were
to point to a student crossing the campus
and say to an average companion, 'Yon
der is a saint,* that average companion
would intuitively picture a man separated
from his fellows, dressed in the uniform
of holiness and the possessor of a certain
spirit that would characterize the man in
the temple of old. 'God, I thank thee that
I am not as other men are.'
THE TRUE IDEAL.
"The reputation of this great university
is not made by the strength of her fac
ulty. Each and every one of you who
goes forth from her walls to take up life's
task, t<* do the duty that lies next to you,
to do it honorably and faithfully, to take
your place in the national life and to en
ter upon the rights of your citizenship â
upon you will rest the great responsibllity
of maintaining the name of your alma
mater. Nor can this be done by mere
cleverness, for behind mental ability
there must shine the light of a strong,
true, pure character. A clever man may
not be a good man, even as a good man
may not be a clever man; but goodness is
ever first in order. Therefore it is that
to complete an education a man must
know where he stands relatively as be
tween the life that now is and the more
abundant life beyond; or, if you prefer to
make it so, between the*natural life now
and that higher spiritual life which is
yours by the truth of birth and by the
birth of truth.

LONDON MARKET
ENJOYS A BOOM
Polly was at least 25 years old. How
much older than that she may have been
is purely a matter of conjecture, as she
talked of everything but : her own age.
Ever since Mrs. Oelrichs was a child she
had Polly for a pet. The parrot was one
oi the members of the Fair household in
San Francisco. She traveled on the spe
cial car Cupid in which Mr. and Mrs.
Oelrichs made their bridal tour across
the continent. ' â¢
. Sorrow served the' household to-day
when it was known that Polly had drawn
her last difficult breath and had winked
her eye in vain at the grim fowler. The
bird had preed her feathers and had tried
in vain to rise to her feet. Her plumage
had lost its luster and her eyes were
devoid of that familiar sparkle, yet she
made a brave effort. -
, Arrangements for the funeral of Polly
are now almost complete, although it is
too early at present to give the full de
tails. . With plumage composed, with the
eyelids closed and tlie^ beak,- firmly com
pressed, Polly now lies in state. A box
carefully prepared will receive the form
and to-morrow morning it Is expected
that ornithological retainer of the houses
of Pair and Oelrichs will be conveyed' to
the Rhode Island watering place. The
interment will be on Mrs. . Oelrichs' es
tate. "'
NEW YORK, May 25.â Death to-day
claimed Polly, the -parrot which for a
quarter of a/'/century had been a pet in
the family, of Mrs. Herman Oelrichs. To
morrow that ancient and parti-colored
bird will be borne to her last resting
place!. Newport will furnish the sepulcher
and by the sea will she find repose. .

UNION, S. C, May 25.â Six persons
were killed and several were injured by
a tornado that swept this section of the
State this afternoon.
Dead:
Miss Sallie'Hart of Union.
Miss Annie Lawson of Union.
Mrs. Maxey Sims of Union.
Vera Sims'.' :, Â¦
Miss JLawson, killed at Jonesville.
One person, unknown, at Pacdlet.
Injured: Maxey Sims, broken fingers;
Lee Sims, internally injured; William
Mabrey, fractured shoulder and bruised;
Mrs. Mabrey, bruised; two children of
Mrs. Mabrey, slightly injured.
Two storms, one from the north and an
other from the southwest, met near here
with terrific force. The storm was pre
ceded by a heavy, rain. One wing of the
tornado passed along Main street and
blew in several store fronts, doing much
damage to stocks. . : â¢ â¢
Knitting Mill Hill,~ south of the town,
felt the full force of the tornado, which
blew down the school house and two resi
dences, converting them, literally into
kindling wood. The occupants ran from,
one of the houses just before it went to
pieces and took refuge in another near
.by, but this house was also crushed to
splinters. In this house Sallie Hart and
Annie Lawson were instantly killed, Mrs. N
Maxey Sims died in an hour and her little
daughter, Vera, died soon afterward.
Maxey Sims escaped with broken fingers.
Lee Sims received several cuts on the
head from falling timbers and was in
ternally injured. Mr. Mabrey sustained
a fractured shoulder and his legs were
badly burned by a stove falling on him.
Mrs. Lee Siiris received a bad but. not
fatal cut on the head. Mrs. Mabrey's two
children escaped with slight injuries. It
took some time to .get the- victims from
the debris. Every physician in town was
called Â«and they were assisted by the cit
izens in relieving the sufferers as much
as possible.
Jonesville reports that : the storm
wrought much damage there and that a
Miss Lawson was killed.
Pacolet also reports one killed and sev
eral injured. Â¦'.-â¢Â¦ - "â¢
The property damage in Union is $50,000.

Witherup's neck was broken by the fall.

KANSAS CITY, Mo., May 25.â Jesse
Johnson, a negro, who murdered M. D.
McCray, postmaster at Rensaller, Rails
County, Mo., on April 28. and who was
taken to the Paris jail on May 2 to pre
vent his being lynched, was brought here
to-night for safety. Johnson was placed
in the jail at New London, Mo., after
the murder. A mob stormed the jail on
the same*night, intent on lynching him,
but the officers had anticipated the act
and removed him to Paris. During the
excitement at Paris last night he was
placed on the train for Kansas City.
Whitecotton was swept aside and in a
few minutes Wltherup was secured. The
murderer made no resistance and in reply
to questions confessed his guilt. The
father and brother of the victim, were in
the mob and assisted In escorting the
murderer to the bridge a quarter of a
mile away.
The mob then quickly reappeared, sur
rounded the jail again and with sledge
hammers battered" down the big iron
doors. This was the work of but a few
minutes. James H. "Whitecotton, State
Representative, rushed into the jail as
soon as the doors gave way and made a
stirring appeal to the avengers to desist
and allow the law to take its course. The
leaders wavered for a minute and became
quiet and probably wo'uld have spared the
prisoner's life but for the frenzied cries
for vengeance set up by those in the rear.
The mob remained in front of the jail
for nearly two hours, parleying with the
officers, and finally marching away. The
officers remained on guard, but believed
that the mob had disbanded. About 2
o'clock several men approached and en
gaged the Sheriff and his deputies in con
versation. -Then, suddenly, before the
officers could defend themselves, they
were seized, disarmed and carried away.
The mob went at once to the jail, but
was held back for more than an hour by
Sheriff James W. Clark and Deputies
Martin Clark and Polk Masterson, who
stood before the entrance to the jail stock
ade with drawn revolvers and threatened
to shoot the first man who attempted to
force an entrance.

To Visit San Francisco
Without eeeing the Diamond Palate would
be . like visiting Europe without seeing:
Paris. It is a leading feature of San
Francisco. It is a marvel of beauty an-3
elegance. It Is unquestionably the most
magnificent jewelry emporium in the
world â the splendid conception of a mas
ter mind, a controlling genius. Artistic
taste and skill challenge competition here.
To be appreciated it must be seen. Every
visitor and resident should go and exam
ine the marvels of genius at No. 221
Montgomery Ft. A. Andrews, proprietor.*
