BODY TAKEN TO CHAPEL.
Dr. Leavitt followed with a short
prayer, and then the flowers that covered
the head of the casket were removed so
that the friends might have a last look
on the face cf the man they honored
by their presence. A long line was
formed, and for t?n minutes people pass
ed, viewing the calm face of the dead.
Dr. Leavitt read the funeral service
cf the Unitarian church, and then intro
duced Edward J. Livernash, who delivered
the eulogy. The speaker was greatly af
fected by the death of his friend, and
lie spoke from the heart. His words were
words of sorrow, and the language that
flowed from his trembling lips denoted
th-j grief under which he was laboring.
Mr. Livernash's tribute to the memory
of William De Mott Dressier was a sin
cere one. He tcld of the qualities pos
sessed by the deceased, of his kindness
of heart and sincertiy of purpose. He
dwelt on the good he had done in the
community by his forceful writings and
of his modesty. He expressed the great
sorrow all felt at his untimely taking
off and of the loss sustained bj,- the com
munity. In closing his eulogy Mr. Liv
ernash's eyes were dimmed with tears,
and as he uttered the words "Good by â
good by, 'Jake,' old man,"' there was
hardly a dry eye in the gathering.
The funeral of William De Mott Dress
ier, the well-known journalist, who died
last Friday morning, took place yester
day. Friends of the deceased gathered
round the steps of the chapel at Odd
Fellows' Cemetery, where the ser
vices were held, and with uncovered heads
listened to the reading of the Scriptures
by Rev. Bradford Leavitt, pastor of the
First Unitarian Church.
The ceremonies were simple but im
pressive. In the gathering that sur
rounded the caske: were men in all
walks of life. Judges, city officials, phy
sicians, lawyers, newspaper men and
friends of the deceased were present to
pay a last tribute of respect to the dead,
tiorrow was depicted on the faces of
those who stood by the bier, for they
all loved the man who was taken from
tfceir midst.
Tne stairs of the chapel were covered
with magnificent floral tributes from his
fellow workers and friends. A vacant
chair, with the words "City Editor,"
Â¦was a tribute from friends. Other
handsome floral pieces attested to the
esteem in which "he was held by those
who knew him intimately.
DELIVERS ELOQUENT EULOGY.
Preceding Mills' lecture Mr?. Emllv
Conklin Lucey sang several songs appro
priate to the occasion.
In its social aspect*, the Chrijstlan gospel. If
practically applied, could scarcely be Improved.
It teaches men not only to love their neighbors,
but to love them even better than .themselves.
It says that if all men are children of God
they are true brothers, one of another. "Chris
tianity is the religion of love made easy." It
gives also a great conception of the world and
the position of humanity, in it. It doeÂ» not
really teach, as has sometimes been proclaim
ed In its name, that the chief object of man
is to escape from a ruined and sinking: wreck,
nor even to save the wreck, as tome have
taught, but rather that it is our duty to help
the world on to a practical justice. The pas
sion that was manifested In the great crusades
and afterwards by the building of the magnif
icent cathedrals of Europe, needs to-day to be
intelligently applied to the organization Of hu
man society. If this cannot be done in the
name of Christianity, then Christianity . will
perish from the earth and a new â¢ and better
form of religious expression will take its place.
To ray that greater men are yet to Come Is
"not to detract from the majestic character of
Christ, but to affirm the omnipotence of God."
The religion of brotherly love will outlast any
name that now we know, and be perfected In
a new hÂ«aven and a new earth wherein will
dwell justice. ...
Christianity srives to the world to-day a grreat
conception of man and his relation to God and
to his fellow men. It regards him as God's
child, but loving unworthily like the prodigal,
and says to him. "Return unto God and he will
return unto you." It says that God is spirit,
that the true man also must be Epirlt, and that
theenre of all his defects will be manifested
if he joins himself in the closest communion
to God. . Â¦ .
Jesus had this personal conception of a
righteous God in whom righteousness was iden
tical with love. He carried the old prophetic
conception to its limit, until God became to him
a father and nothing: else. For him the way
to know God was through love. It was a nat
ural thins that Paul, meditating on the life
and death of Jesus, should have seen him as a
perfect illustration "i this and announced him
as the incarnation of love, and the complete
revelation of God and should have satisfied his
legal mind by attemtping to show how God
could be righteous and yet forgive the wicked.
So he said. God himself bears the punishment
for man's sin. Poetically this was true, and
the Idea of love pouring out its life at. the de
mand of Justice for the service of humanity
can never fail in some form to exist In a world
that believes in a loving God. Much 6o~called
infidelity is simply the demand that God shall
be recognized as love and nothing- but love.
1 Benjamin Fay Mills delivered a lecture
last night in Golden Gate -Hall on "The
Transient and Permanent in Christian
ity." His subject was divided into the
origin of Christianity, its history, its doc
trines and what remains of value for the
present and the future. He said that lit
tle reliable history concerning the origin
of Christianity existed, but that there
was a great mass of tradition enveloping
it, and that one of the most significant
facts about its growth was that it never
gained any victories in the land where it
had its birth.
Regarding the teachings of Jesus and
Paul and the true aims of Christianity
Mills said:

The Rev. j Dr. Adams delivered- a ser
mon, "Remember the Days of Old,", which
gave him .the opportunity ' to relate in
masterful spirit the many deeds of valor
of the soldiers of the United. States. The
lecturer not -only spoke Â¦ of '.the Civil -"War
and the valiant deeds performed, but told
briefly the cause that led up to the war.
He' thanked t God that all differences had
been obliterated and : that to-day there
was no South nor, was there a North; but
all . were united as one. The war with
Spain had shown the eagerness of those
of the South to get first to the battle-field
with the object of defending the old flag.
Dr. Adams spoke of those who, fell in'de

I am pleased to be with you all on this
merry day. I am always happy to meet my
fellow-laborers wherever they may bÂ«. Thie
Is the first time that I have enjoyed the op
portunity of meeting and greeting on an occa
sion of this kind any cf the union and working
men of Alameda County. , I had Intended to'
do bo before this, but business and engage
ments elsewhere prevented. As â¢ you are all
probably aware, I owe the present position I
occupy as Mayor of San Francleco, In a very
great measure to the fidelity arid loyalty of
cisco. The Mayor was accompa
nied by his family and greatly enjoyed
the outing with the . tonsorlallsts. His
Honor addresesd the assemblage in the
dancing pavilion at 3 o'clock in a talk
that was brief, happy and appropriate to
the occasion. Cheers greeted the speaker
when he appeared and when he left the
platform. Mayor Schmitz said in part: .
